About the second edition of the
AnaCredit Manual
1 The AnaCredit Reporting Manual (the “Manual”) has been updated. The previous
2 version dated back to November 2016.
3 Highlights of this edition
4 The Manual has been updated to acknowledge and incorporate the additional
5 explanations provided in the Q&As published on the ECB’s website between July
6 2017 and December 2018.
7 The updated Manual also incorporates a number of adjustments to address previous
8 inconsistencies. At the same time, the structure has been reorganised and
9 streamlined. Specifically, some explanatory paragraphs have been moved to
10 different parts of the Manual, and cross-references have been added with a view to
11 facilitating consistency and readability. Moreover, references to the implementing
12 technical standards (ITS) on supervisory reporting have been updated throughout
13 the Manual to keep them in line with the amendments to Commission Implementing
14 Regulation (EU) No 680/2014 (the “amended ITS”).
15 Finally, the format and the language style have been aligned throughout the whole
16 text.
17 Marked changes
18 Marked changes: changes have In order to help trace where amendments other than additional cross-references or
been annotated with revision marks
19 minor textual adjustments have been made throughout the text, changes are
20 indicated in the margin of the document, with a brief explanation. Furthermore,
21 where relevant, hyperlinks to the published Q&As are provided there. The Q&As,
22 although rendered redundant by the updated Manual, are still accessible on the ECB
23 website.

AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 4
31 May 2019
1 About the Manual
1.1 Introduction
1 This Manual presents detailed information and guidance concerning AnaCredit
2 reporting requirements as set out in the AnaCredit Regulation1.
3 The Manual explains the methodology underpinning data collection and the data
4 model, and includes guidance on the preferred approach that may be taken in cases
5 where the respective ECB legal act leaves scope for different interpretations.
6 The Manual contains no additional requirements and has no binding legal status, as
7 it merely aims to provide guidance by clarifying and providing examples related to
8 the requirements and definitions previously laid down in the AnaCredit Regulation.
9 The AnaCredit Regulation is the sole legally binding act.
10 The requirements of AnaCredit establish a common granular credit dataset shared
11 among the Eurosystem members (referred to as the AnaCredit database) and
12 comprising input data of all euro area Member States and any other states
13 participating in AnaCredit on a voluntary basis.
14 With a view to creating a common database, it is of the utmost importance that
15 concepts, definitions and reporting practices are aligned across countries to ensure a
16 rigorous methodological background and accurate comparable international
17 information.
1.2 Who should read the Manual
18 The information in the Manual may be of interest to reporting agents, data compilers
19 and users of the AnaCredit database.
20 The Manual also provides essential information for national authorities in charge of
21 collecting the information as a part of a broader national reporting framework, with a
22 view to ensuring consistency between the AnaCredit requirements and any related
23 national extensions thereof.
1.3 Which reporting requirements are covered in this Manual
24 Only the common requirements as The Manual only covers the reporting requirements laid down in the AnaCredit
set out in the Regulation are
25 Regulation.
covered
No national extensions covered
1 Regulation (EU) 2016/867 of the European Central Bank of 18 May 2016 on the collection of
granular credit and credit risk data (ECB/2016/13) (OJ L 144, 1.6.2016, p. 44).
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 5
31 May 2019
1 The Manual does not cover any other reporting requirements; this relates in
2 particular to any additional requirements that are part of a broader national reporting
3 framework and extend the reporting of credit and credit risk data beyond the scope
4 outlined in the AnaCredit Regulation. If relevant, any such additional requirements
5 will be determined and appropriately communicated by national central banks
6 (NCBs).
1.4 Scope of national implementations
7 National extensions With a view to ensuring efficient reporting and adequate interoperability with other
8 existing or new reporting frameworks, NCBs may collect the information to be
9 transmitted to the AnaCredit database as part of a broader national reporting
10 framework. Consequently, NCBs may extend the reporting of granular credit and
11 credit risk data beyond the scope outlined in the AnaCredit Regulation, for their own
12 statutory purposes, in line with relevant national law.
13 Similarly, NCBs decide on the reporting format and timeliness in which they receive
14 the data from reporting agents.
15 In no case does this Manual cover any national extensions of the common reporting
16 requirements, or the format of the reporting or the timeliness with which reporting
17 agents send data to their relevant NCBs.
18 Relevant information on national extensions and the timeliness or format of the
19 reporting is provided by the relevant NCBs.
20 Areas of national discretion In addition to national extensions, there are also requirements set out in the
21 AnaCredit Regulation that are left at NCBs’ discretion.
22 Generally, requirements that are left at NCBs’ discretion are those that may be rolled
23 out differently (or not rolled out at all) in different reporting Member States.
24 Any such requirements are presented and in principle explained in this Manual, i.e.
25 assuming the baseline scenario that reporting agents submit all data attributes listed
26 in Annex I of the AnaCredit Regulation. In other words, for the purpose of the
27 Manual, the requirements are explained as if NCBs had decided not to deviate from
28 the baseline scenario in which all the requirements are required in full. In practice,
29 however, not all of the data attributes may actually be required. The Manual does not
30 provide any information about the actual implementation of such requirements by
31 NCBs.
32 Revision mark: editorial changes Reporting agents and other stakeholders are invited to contact the relevant NCB for
33 information regarding the national implementation of AnaCredit.

1.5 Structure of the Manual
35 The Manual is organised into the following parts:
36 Part I explains the general AnaCredit methodology and provides information about
37 the various steps involved in determining the reporting population and the credit
38 instruments subject to reporting and setting up the reporting, including a general
39 description of the underlying data model. It also explains the reduced data
40 requirements as stipulated by Article 7 of the AnaCredit Regulation and the
41 application of derogations for small reporting agents.
42 Part II describes all datasets and data attributes of AnaCredit data collection in detail
43 and provides specific reporting instructions.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 7
31 May 2019
1 Part III presents various case studies and in particular covers special scenarios that
2 require more in-depth explanations.
3 Finally, the Annexes provide additional information relevant for reporting agents such
4 as certain data validation rules.
1.5.1 Part I – general methodology, reporting principles and rules
5 Chapter 1 provides information about the content and intended use of this Manual.
6 Chapter 2 focuses on reporting agents, in particular the scope in terms of the
7 reporting population, and provides a detailed explanation of the entities subject to
8 reporting (reporting agents). The scope in terms of the reference population
9 (observed agents) is then discussed. The reporting obligations of reporting agents
10 are also described, including the meaning of reporting on an individual basis or when
11 there is a change in the population of reporting agents. An explanation of the
12 applicable accounting standard to be used by reporting agents is also provided.
13 Chapter 3 describes counterparties in the context of AnaCredit and provides a
14 definition both of counterparties directly involved in instruments and of counterparties
15 acting as protection providers. A specific section is dedicated to counterparties
16 affiliated with debtors and protection providers.
17 Chapter 4 describes the scope of AnaCredit data collection in terms of instruments
18 covered and focuses on specific issues related to eligible instruments, in particular
19 the activity of observed agents as creditors or servicers, instruments relevant for the
20 creditor, held or serviced instruments, types of counterparties involved and the types
21 of instrument that give rise to credit risk.
22 Chapter 5 explains the different criteria that trigger a reporting obligation with regard
23 to reporting reference dates and the reference period, eligible instruments and
24 debtors’ commitment amounts. The chapter also provides an explanation of when a
25 given instrument is reportable under AnaCredit.
26 Chapter 6 addresses questions related to the concept of credit and the underlying
27 data model. Specifically, credit is conceptually modelled by distinguishing the
28 instrument entity, the protection entity and the counterparty entity. The reportable
29 datasets are then defined on the basis of the conceptual model. Mutual relationships
30 between the reportable datasets are also discussed along with the different methods
31 of reporting data and the reporting frequencies.
32 Chapter 7 discusses the specific statistical reporting requirements related to
33 counterparty and credit data, as set out in Annexes II and III of the AnaCredit
34 Regulation.
35 Finally, Chapter 8 details the circumstances in which derogations and reduced
36 reporting frequency for small reporting agents can be applied.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 8
31 May 2019
1.5.2 Part II – datasets and data attributes
1 Part II specifies all the reportable datasets by focusing on the specific data attributes
2 for each reported dataset.
3 In particular, the reporting frequency is discussed for every dataset, and the
4 reporting qualification, definition, applicable values, general reporting instructions
5 and some examples of specific cases are provided for every data attribute.
1.5.3 Part III – case studies
6 Part III analyses specific use cases and examples of particular scenarios. A set of
7 case studies illustrates the issues and challenges encountered. The explanations of
8 how to tackle these issues in the context of AnaCredit reporting help both reporting
9 agents and data analysts to properly reflect the economic substance of the
10 information at hand.
11 Revision mark: editorial changes Part III covers reporting instructions for cases with a given central element, such as
12 securitisations, instruments under a multi-debtor/product structure (credit limit
13 structures), project finance loans, factoring transactions, reverse repurchase
14 agreements and syndicated loans and other multi-creditor instruments. It also
15 provides detailed examples of complete reports.

complementary Q&A process

29 communicates this back to the requester – adjusting the response in line with the
30 national reporting framework if applicable. A selection of these Q&As is published on
31 the ECB’s website on an ongoing basis.

1.8 Other relevant information
4 Besides this Manual there is also additional AnaCredit documentation which
5 provides a consistent and comprehensive overview of the reporting requirements
6 from both a methodological and a technical point of view. In addition to the AnaCredit
7 Regulation and the AnaCredit Guideline2, the RIAD Guideline3 provides a description
8 of dataflow frameworks for the enhanced Register of Institutions and Affiliates
9 Database. Finally, in light of Section 1.4 above, available documentation at a
10 national level is also relevant.
11 The AnaCredit Guideline is a legally binding document addressed to NCBs’ that will
12 accompany the AnaCredit Regulation. It details NCBs’ obligations to report credit
13 and credit risk data and reference data to the ECB.
14 Other documents related to AnaCredit within the scope of the Single Data Dictionary
15 (SDD) and the Banks’ Integrated Reporting Dictionary (BIRD)4 also provide
16 information relevant to AnaCredit.
17 The Single Data Dictionary provides the technical specification for the dataset flows
18 between NCBs and the ECB. It only covers the dataflow between NCBs and the
19 ECB, and not the dataflow between reporting agents and NCBs. The latter will be
20 established by the respective NCBs, and reporting agents are advised to consult
21 their NCBs accordingly.
22 Revision mark: editorial changes The BIRD may be used as complementary information. The BIRD has been
are applied
23 designed to alleviate the reporting burden for the banks. The BIRD’s contents,
24 published on the BIRD website, comprise a precise description of the data which
25 should be extracted from the banks’ internal IT systems to derive reports demanded
26 by authorities. In addition to this, there will be clearly defined transformation rules to
27 be applied to the data extracted from the banks’ internal IT systems in order to
28 produce a specific final regulatory figure. The scope of the BIRD also covers the
29 common AnaCredit requirements.

2 Reporting agents and observed agents
1 The first section of this chapter discusses the scope of reporting in terms of entities
2 subject to reporting, which form the reporting population. The second section
3 addresses the scope of reporting in terms of the reference population and provides a
4 detailed description of institutional units and observed agents. Finally, the third
5 section describes the reporting obligations of reporting agents, where the meaning of
6 reporting on an individual basis and the use of special reporting values are
7 explained.
2.1 General concepts
2.1.1 The concept of credit institutions
8 Pursuant to Article 1(18) of the AnaCredit Regulation, credit institution has the same
9 meaning as defined in Article 4(1)(1) of Regulation (EU) No 575/2013 (hereinafter
10 referred to as “the CRR”)5.
11 Revision mark: editorial changes Article 4(1)(1) of the CRR defines a credit institution as “an undertaking the business
are applied
12 of which is to take deposits or other repayable funds from the public and to grant
13 credits for its own account”. Exemptions laid down in other legal frameworks, such
14 as in Directive 2013/36/EU (hereinafter referred to as “the CRD IV”)6, do not apply.
15 Subsidiaries of credit institutions which meet the above-mentioned definition of a
16 credit institution are credit institutions in their own right.
2.1.2 The concept of institutional units
17 As regards the organisation of AnaCredit reporting, the AnaCredit Regulation uses
18 the concept of an institutional unit.
19 Pursuant to Article 1(3) of the AnaCredit Regulation, “institutional unit” has the same
20 meaning as defined in paragraphs 2.12 and 2.13 of Annex A to Regulation (EU)
5 Regulation (EU) No 575/2013 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2013 on
prudential requirements for credit institutions and investment firms and amending Regulation
(EU) No 648/2012 (OJ L 176, 27.6.2013, p. 1).
6 Directive 2013/36/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 June 2013 on access
to the activity of credit institutions and the prudential supervision of credit institutions and
investment firms, amending Directive 2002/87/EC and repealing Directives 2006/48/EC and
2006/49/EC (OJ L 176, 27.6.2013, p. 338).

1 No 549/2013 (hereinafter referred to as “ESA 2010”)7 – refer to Table 1 for the
2 definition of an institutional unit.
Table 1: Paragraph 2.12 of Annex A to Regulation (EU) No 549/2013
Definition: an institutional unit is an economic entity characterised by decision-making
autonomy in the exercise of its principal function. A resident unit is regarded as
constituting an institutional unit in the economic territory where it has its centre of
predominant economic interest if it has decision-making autonomy and either keeps a
complete set of accounts or is able to compile a complete set of accounts.
To have autonomy of decision in respect of its principal function, an entity must be:
• entitled to own goods and assets in its own right; it will be able to exchange the
ownership of goods and assets in transactions with other institutional units;
• able to take economic decisions and engage in economic activities for which it is
responsible and accountable at law;
• able to incur liabilities on its own behalf, to take on other obligations or further
commitments and to enter into contracts; and
• able to draw up a complete set of accounts, comprised of accounting records
covering all its transactions carried out during the accounting period, as well as a
balance sheet of assets and liabilities.

7 of the institution. There may only be one institutional unit in any given country. The
8 term institutional unit is a central concept of AnaCredit as pursuant to Article 1(10) of
9 the AnaCredit Regulation all counterparties required to be reported to AnaCredit are
10 defined as institutional units.
2.1.3 Institutional units of credit institutions
11 A credit institution consists of one or more institutional units. A distinction is made
12 between the domestic part of a credit institution and its foreign branches.
13 A credit institution always comprises the domestic part of a credit institution.
14 If a credit institution also conducts business in countries other than the country in
15 which it is established, the credit institution also comprises foreign branches.

2.1.4 Subsidiaries of credit institutions
1 A subsidiary of a (parent) credit institution does not form an institutional unit of the
2 parent credit institution.
3 A subsidiary is a legal entity in its own right with its own institutional units.
2.2 Reporting Member States, reporting agents and
observed agents in AnaCredit
2.2.1 The distinction between the reporting population and the reference
population
4 AnaCredit distinguishes between the populations of reporting agents (the reporting
5 population) and observed agents (the reference population).
6 The rationale for distinguishing between reporting agents and observed agents is
7 that AnaCredit is designed to collect credit data with a view to obtaining a complete
8 picture of the credit exposures of reporting agents in the reporting Member States,
9 taking into account the area of economic activity of the reporting agent on the one
10 hand, and its credit exposure in a specific country on the other. Subsequently, by
11 linking observed agents with reporting agents, data are collected on loans granted
12 both by resident credit institutions, irrespective of whether they are provided directly
13 by the (domestic parts of the) credit institutions or indirectly via their foreign branches
14 (resident or non-resident in a reporting Member State), and by resident foreign
15 branches of non-resident credit institutions.
2.2.2 Reporting Member States
16 Revision mark: Section 2.2.2 is In line with Article 1(1) of the AnaCredit Regulation, a reporting Member State is:
added covering the definition of
17 “reporting Member State” as laid • a Member State of the European Union whose currency is the euro;
down in the AnaCredit Regulation
18 • a Member State of the European Union whose currency is not the euro that
19 has joined AnaCredit on a voluntary basis.
20 A Member State whose currency is not the euro may decide to become a reporting
21 Member State by incorporating the provisions of the AnaCredit Regulation into its
22 national law or otherwise imposing relevant reporting requirements in accordance
23 with its national law. This may include, in particular, a Member State that participates
24 in the Single Supervisory Mechanism (SSM) via close cooperation in accordance
25 with Article 7 of Council Regulation (EU) No 1024/2013.

2.2.5 Observed agents
7 Pursuant to Article 1(9) of the AnaCredit Regulation, an observed agent is an
8 institutional unit whose activity as creditor or servicer is reported by the reporting
9 agent. The observed agent is either:
10 • the domestic part of the reporting agent, i.e. the institutional unit resident in the
11 same country as the reporting agent of which it forms part; or
12 • a reporting agent’s foreign branch, resident in a reporting Member State; or
13 • a reporting agent’s foreign branch, non-resident in a reporting Member State.

2.3 Reporting obligations of reporting agents
2.3.1 General principle
1 The reporting obligations of reporting agents pursuant to the AnaCredit Regulation
2 are without prejudice to any other reporting requirements in accordance with national
3 law or other reporting frameworks.
2.3.2 Actual reporting agents
4 The ECB does not provide a list of the credit institutions or foreign branches of credit
5 institutions that fall within the scope of AnaCredit.
6 Revision mark: a reference to the Nevertheless, the relevant NCBs inform reporting agents about the reporting
AnaCredit Regulation is removed
7 obligations before the first reporting reference date for which such agents report data
8 to AnaCredit.
2.3.3 Reporting on an individual basis
9 As set out in Article 6(2) of the AnaCredit Regulation, resident credit institutions
10 report data in relation to all their observed agents.
11 Resident foreign branches of credit institutions report on their own activity as creditor
12 or servicer.
13 Reporting on an individual basis means that loans exchanged between institutional
14 units of a legal entity (for example, between the domestic part and foreign branches)
15 are in principle subject to reporting.
16 Pursuant to Article 3(3), all reporting agents report data to their respective NCB,
17 which is uniquely determined by the country of residence of the reporting agent.
18 Specifically, where a resident credit institution has foreign branches, data concerning
19 all foreign branches of the credit institution are identified individually (separately) –
20 by the counterparty identifier of the institutional unit – and are reported by the
21 reporting agent (which is the credit institution resident in a reporting Member State).
22 The reporting agent reports the data to the relevant NCB of the Member State where
23 the credit institution is established.

agents

27 reporting agent in its own right and reports to the NCB of the Member State in which
28 the observed agent (i.e. the foreign branch) is resident.

AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 25
31 May 2019
Chart 5: Reporting agent’s obligations – perspective of a resident credit institution
Depending on the particular situation, the reporting agent is the credit institution and/or
the resident foreign branch. A total of three cases arise:
1. The credit institution has no foreign branch – in this case the credit institution is
the reporting agent and reports on its own activity as creditor or servicer (i.e.
the credit institution is the only observed agent). The reporting agent reports
the data of the observed agent to the NCB in the country where the credit
institution is established.
2. The credit institution has a foreign branch resident in a reporting Member State
–in this case the following applies:
(a) The credit institution is a reporting agent and reports on the activity as
creditor or servicer of (i) the one observed agent being the domestic
part of the credit institution and (ii) the other observed agent being the
foreign branch of the credit institution; both sets of data are reported to
the NCB in the country where the credit institution is established.
(b) The resident foreign branch is also a reporting agent and reports on its
own activity as creditor or servicer (i.e. the foreign branch is the
observed agent). The reporting agent reports the data of the observed
agent to the NCB in the Member State where the foreign branch is
located.
3. The credit institution has a foreign branch which is not resident in a reporting
Member State – in this case the credit institution is a reporting agent and
reports on the activity as creditor or servicer of (i) the one observed agent
being the domestic part of the credit institution and (ii) the other observed
agent being the foreign branch of the credit institution; both sets of data are
reported to the NCB in the country where the credit institution is established.
The non-resident foreign branch is not a reporting agent in its own right.
If the reporting agent has both resident and non-resident foreign branches, the data for
the domestic part of the credit institution only need to be reported once (combination of
cases 2 and 3).
Please note that in line with Article 6(4) of the AnaCredit Regulation, the respective
NCB may decide not to collect any data for foreign branches located in countries which
are not a reporting Member State.
This is an overview of a reporting agent’s obligations to report on its observed agents’
activities as creditor or servicer, using the example of a credit institution established in a
reporting Member State.
The flow chart presents the comprehensive reporting obligation of the resident credit
institution and possibly of its foreign branches, depending on the composition of the
credit institution.

From the perspective of the Banque de France (BdF), the credit institution is the
reporting agent and reports to the BdF the data relating to its observed agents. The
data are reported separately for each observed agent:
• data for domestic part of the credit institution established in France;

• data for the foreign branch located in country X.

AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 29

countries which are not a reporting Member State.
The credit institution does not report data relating to either of the two subsidiaries to the

If we consider the credit institution’s situation from the perspective of the Italian national
central bank, the following applies:
• The foreign branch in Italy is a reporting agent in Italy and reports data on its own
activity as creditor or servicer to the Banca d’Italia (BdI).
• The foreign branch in Italy is not a reporting agent for the subsidiary in Italy.
• The foreign branch in Italy is not a reporting agent for any entity outside Italy.
• The subsidiary in Italy is a reporting agent in its own right in Italy and reports data
on its own activity as creditor or servicer to the BdI. In other words the subsidiary
itself – or the domestic part thereof together with the subsidiary’s foreign branches,
if it has any – is its observed agent).
• The subsidiary in Italy is not a reporting agent for the foreign branch in Italy.
• The subsidiary in Italy is not a reporting agent for any entity in country X.
Finally, since country X is not a reporting Member State, neither the subsidiary in
country X nor the foreign branch in country X is a reporting agent.

2.3.5 Reporting standards to be applied by reporting agents
5 NCBs will inform reporting agents Pursuant to Article 13(2) of the AnaCredit Regulation, NCBs decide when and how
about the timeliness of reporting
6 often they receive data from reporting agents in order to meet their reporting
7 deadlines to the ECB, and inform the reporting agents accordingly.
8 Specifically and according to Annex V of the AnaCredit Regulation, when fulfilling
9 their reporting obligations in the context of AnaCredit reporting, reporting agents
10 satisfy reporting standards that include but are not limited to the following:
(a) reporting to NCBs is timely and within the deadlines set by the relevant
NCB;
(b) statistical reports take their form and format from the technical reporting
requirements set by the relevant NCB;
(c) the technical specifications for data transmission to the relevant NCB are
followed.
11 NCBs also determine whether third parties may submit reports on behalf of reporting
12 agents.
2.3.6 Relevant accounting standards
13 Point 6.2 of Annex I, Template 2, of the AnaCredit Regulation stipulates that the
14 accounting data of an observed agent that are reported to AnaCredit comply with the
15 accounting standard used by the observed agent’s legal entity.
16 Specifically, the accounting standard relevant for any observed agent is the
17 accounting standard used by the credit institution of which the institutional unit (i.e.
18 the observed agent) is part. The accounting standard is relevant for AnaCredit
19 reporting in general and not only in relation to the data attributes in the accounting
20 dataset referred to in Annex I of the AnaCredit Regulation (cf. Section 6 for more
21 information regarding reportable datasets).
22 This means in particular that if the observed agent’s legal entity is subject to
23 Regulation (EU) 2015/534 (ECB/2015/13), the data are reported in accordance with
24 either International Financial Reporting Standards (IFRS) or national generally
25 accepted accounting principles (GAAP) – depending on which standards are applied
26 by the legal entity to report its individual FINREP templates to its supervisor.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 31
31 May 2019
2.3.7 Counterparty identifiers to be used in relation to reporting and
observed agents
1 The AnaCredit Regulation requires that both the reporting agent which reports the
2 data and the observed agent whose activity as creditor or servicer is reported be
3 identifiable. For this reason, all the data reported, with the exception of the
4 counterparty reference data, include the identifier of the reporting agent and the
5 observed agent.
6 The identifiers are counterparty identifiers. In the primary reporting (i.e. between
7 reporting agents and the relevant NCB), the identifiers are uniquely assigned at the
8 level of the reporting agent. In the secondary reporting (i.e. between NCBs and the
9 ECB), the identifiers are assigned by the relevant NCB, ensuring that they are
10 uniquely assigned at national level.
11 Whether the same identifiers are used in the primary and secondary reporting
12 depends on national arrangements.

3 Counterparties in AnaCredit
1 This chapter describes counterparties in the context of AnaCredit and provides
2 definitions of both counterparties directly involved in instruments and counterparties
3 acting as protection providers. Furthermore, a specific section is dedicated to
4 counterparties affiliated with debtors and protection providers. More detailed
5 guidance on counterparties is provided in Chapters 6 and 12 of Part II of the Manual,
6 which deal specifically with the counterparty reference data and the counterparty-
7 instrument data (with data on the counterparty role).
3.1 Identification of counterparties relevant in AnaCredit
8 In the context of AnaCredit, counterparty means an institutional unit that is a party to
9 an instrument or to a protection item, or has an affiliation with such a party.
10 All counterparties taking any of the The AnaCredit Regulation stipulates that all counterparties (that are not natural
roles in relation to instruments
11 persons) which take any of the following roles are relevant counterparties and are
should be registered in the
12 counterparty reference data, unless registered in the counterparty reference data:
a counterparty is a natural person
13 • the debtor of the instrument;
14 • the creditor of the instrument;
15 • the servicer of the instrument;
16 • the originator of the instrument, if the instrument is subject to securitisation;
17 • the protection provider that provides protection (if any) to the instrument;
18 • the head office undertaking of (a foreign branch that is) a debtor of the
19 instrument or a protection provider that provides protection to the
20 instrument;
21 • the immediate parent undertaking of any debtor of the instrument, or of any
22 protection provider that provides protection to the instrument;
23 • the ultimate parent undertaking of any debtor of the instrument, or of any
24 protection provider that provides protection to the instrument.
25 Revision mark: editorial changes These and only these counterparties are recognised in AnaCredit.
are applied

institutional units and legal entities

28 of their institutional units, i.e. their observed agents and further in Section 3.4 in relation to counterparties other than

4 When identifying counterparties in AnaCredit, the following steps essentially need to
5 be considered:
(a) if reporting an instrument to AnaCredit as at a given reporting reference

6 1. debtor of the instrument;
7 2. creditor of the instrument;
8 3. servicer of the instrument;
9 4. originator, if the instrument is subject to securitisation;

provider of the protection item is identified;
(c) once all counterparties taking the role of debtor or the role of protection
provider have been identified, the following information is required:
10 1. the head office undertaking of the counterparty,8
11 2. the immediate parent undertaking of the counterparty (if any);
12 3. the ultimate parent undertaking of the counterparty (if any);
(d) ultimately, all counterparties identified along steps a), b) and c) are
registered in the counterparty reference data as of the given reporting
reference date;
(e) however, the requirement in point 5(d) above is waived for all
counterparties which are natural persons, because data on natural

Example 1: Creditors vis-à-vis traditional securitisation transactions
A traditional securitisation is a transfer of an instrument (or pool of instruments, or part
thereof) to a financial vehicle corporation (FVC), either by the transfer of legal title or
beneficial interest of the instruments from the originator or through sub-participation
where one or more sub-participants agree to fund an instrument in return for the right to
receive the principal and interest repayments for the instrument.
These are securitisations where the transferee acquires the economic ownership of the
instrument, in other words the legal title or the risk and rewards of the transferred
instruments.
In these securitisations, from the moment of the onward transfer, the transferor (i.e. the
original creditor) ceases to be the creditor of the instrument and the transferee (i.e. the
counterparty that acquires the instrument) becomes the new creditor.
When the original creditor only transfers part of the economic ownership of an
instrument, this counterparty remains the creditor to that part of the instrument that it
retains. In addition, because this transaction implies a true sale of the part of the
instrument that has been transferred, the transferee holds a new instrument in the
amount of the transferred part of the original instrument, where the transferee is the
creditor to this new instrument (i.e. the transferee acquires its economic ownership).
1 Revision mark: a reference to More guidance on creditors is provided in Sections 6.1.2 and 6.4.1 in Part II of the
Part II of the Manual is added
2 Manual.
3.2.2.1 Observed agent acting as creditor
3 Instruments that are held by the observed agent are all those instruments of which
4 the observed agent is the creditor. All such instruments are considered to give rise to
5 credit risk for the observed agent. In other words, the following three expressions are
6 deemed to have the same meaning and are used interchangeably:
7 • instruments held by the observed agent;
8 • instruments in which the observed agent is the creditor;
9 • instruments that give rise to credit risk for the observed agent.
10 Please note that holding an instrument does not imply that the instrument held is an
11 asset of the holder of the instrument – cf. Section 4.4 for more details.

Example 2: “Servicer” responsible for the administrative and financial management
An instrument is originated by a credit institution and then transferred to another
institution. Under the contract between the originating credit institution (the transferor)
and the institution which acquires the instrument (the transferee), the transferee has all
of the decision-making power in respect of the instrument. However, the administrative
processing continues to be performed by the transferor.
Let’s assume that the transferee is a “bad bank” which is not required to report to
AnaCredit. The instrument’s former creditor (i.e. the transferor), which acts as the
servicer of the instrument, is required to report to AnaCredit on the serviced instrument.
The transferor carries out all day-to-day business in respect of the instrument on behalf
of the “bad bank”.
Even though the transferor does not have any decision-making power in respect of the
instrument being serviced, the work that it carries out is included in the definition of
servicing, as explained in Section 6.4.1 in Part II of the Manual. Consequently, it is the
transferor (rather than the new creditor, which is not a reporting agent in the AnaCredit
framework) that is required to report to AnaCredit on the instrument in question.
24 Revision mark: a reference to Please refer to Section 4.2 for more details on the activity of observed agents as
Section 4.2 is added
25 creditors or servicers.

instruments from the originator or through sub-participation;
(b) the credit risk of an instrument or pool of instruments, or part thereof, is

3.3 Counterparties acting as protection providers
3.3.1 Protection provider
25 The term protection provider is defined in Article 1(13) of the AnaCredit Regulation
26 as the counterparty that grants protection against a contractually agreed negative
27 credit event and that bears the credit risk of the negative credit event.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 41
31 May 2019
1 In the context of AnaCredit reporting, every protection item is granted by a protection
2 provider. For example, if real estate serves as protection, the owner of the real
3 estate is the protection provider. If government bonds owned by a legal entity are
4 pledged to secure an instrument (a loan), the legal entity is the protection provider.
5 The protection provider typically differs from the counterparty that issues the
6 protection, although the two do coincide for some types of protection. For instance,
7 guarantors who issue guarantees also act as protection providers –
8 cf. Section 6.2.2.4.
9 A protection provider is clearly distinguished from a debtor, and the distinction
10 between the debtor and the protection provider vis-à-vis an instrument is based on
11 who bears the unconditional obligation to make payments under the instrument. In
12 particular, the debtor is the counterparty that is unconditionally obliged to pay,
13 whereas any counterparty that is conditionally obliged to make payments or to
14 otherwise cede (pledged) protection to the creditor (i.e. upon the occurrence of a
15 certain negative credit event) is the protection provider.
16 For instance, in the case of a loan the debtor is the counterparty to which the loan is
17 granted and which is unconditionally obliged to pay it back. This counterparty is also
18 the debtor if the loan is secured by a third-party financial guarantee, as the guarantor
19 is only conditionally obliged to pay, i.e. the creditor does not have the right to request
20 payment from the guarantor unless the debtor fails to meet its payment obligation.
21 Similarly, the creditor does not have the right to seize the assets of the debtor
22 pledged as protection unless the debtor fails to meet its payment obligation.
23 The distinction between a debtor and a protection provider is further illustrated in
24 Example 3 and results from the way credit is generally modelled in AnaCredit, where
25 the instrument part is separated from the protection part (cf. Section 6.1).
Example 3: Debtor or protection provider?
For the purpose of AnaCredit, the distinction between the debtor of the instrument and
the protection provider that provides a protection item securing the instrument is generally
based on who bears the unconditional obligation to make payments under the instrument.
In particular, the debtor is the counterparty that is unconditionally obliged to make
payments, whereas any counterparty that is obliged only upon the occurrence of a certain
negative credit event is the protection provider.
For a loan secured by a guarantee whereby the guarantor agrees to pay to the creditor if
the debtor defaults, the guarantor is a protection provider as it is only obliged to provide
payment upon the default of the debtor, which is not an unconditional obligation.
However, the credit relationship is recorded between the creditor and the debtor until
such time as the guarantee is called, with the guarantor as protection provider, after the
guarantee is called the guarantor has an unconditional obligation to the creditor and
therefore assumes the role of debtor. At this point, if the guarantor is also a reporting
agent in the context of AnaCredit, the guarantor may also record a claim on the debtor on
its balance sheet. The claim needs to be reported to AnaCredit, where relevant. The
reason for reporting claims arising in this way to AnaCredit is that from the moment the
guarantor pays the original creditor it becomes a (new) creditor of the debtor.

31 May 2019

Example 4: Creditor and protection provider vis-à-vis securitisation transactions
With regard to instruments that are subject to securitisation, a broad distinction is made
between:
• Traditional securitisations (cf. Example 1 above).
In the case of traditional securitisations that are true sales, the transferee becomes the
new creditor of the part of the instruments over which it has acquired economic
ownership.
• Synthetic securitisations – transfer of the credit risk of an instrument (or pool of
instruments, or part thereof), through the use of credit derivatives, guarantees or
any similar mechanism to the investors in the financing instruments issued by an
FVC. FVCs in this category may not fund the instruments whose credit risk is being
transferred.
In the case of synthetic securitisations, the counterparty that assumes the credit risk of
an instrument through the use of credit derivatives, guarantees or any similar
mechanism is not a creditor but a protection provider. Consequently, if the creditor
transfers the instrument to a third party through the use of a guarantee, the transferor is
the originator of the instrument and remains the creditor, while the third party becomes
a protection provider of the instrument.

Part II is added
23 providers.

4 Instruments covered in AnaCredit
1 The purpose of this chapter is to provide a notion of what an instrument is and
2 general reporting guidelines for identifying which instruments (within lending
3 transactions) of credit institutions are subject to AnaCredit reporting.
4 Pursuant to the AnaCredit Regulation, instruments held by observed agents and
5 instruments that are serviced by resident observed agents are considered in the
6 context of AnaCredit reporting, provided that they fulfil the other conditions referred
7 to in Articles 1, 4 and 5 of the AnaCredit Regulation. The criteria triggering the
8 reporting obligations are discussed in Chapter 5.
9 In general, AnaCredit aims to provide an analytical view of credit risk regardless of
10 the financial instrument or the accounting classification. As such, the requirements
11 laid down in the AnaCredit Regulation aim to ensure that reporting agents report to
12 AnaCredit credit and credit risk data for all their observed agents. In this connection,
13 the AnaCredit Regulation distinguishes between (i) instruments held by observed
14 agents and (ii) instruments serviced by observed agents.
15 With a view to identifying the instruments subject to reporting, this chapter focuses
16 only on the counterparty roles of creditor, servicer and debtor that are indispensable
17 for determining the reporting obligation vis-à-vis an instrument. It does not refer to
18 other roles that are generally relevant for AnaCredit. For a complete list of the roles
19 of counterparties please refer to Section 3.1 in the previous chapter.
4.1 The notion of contract and instrument
4.1.1 Contract
20 Pursuant to Article 1(22) of the AnaCredit Regulation, a contract is a legally binding
21 agreement between two or more parties under which one or multiple instruments are
22 created.
23 In the context of AnaCredit, a contract is a credit agreement between two or more
24 entities, with an entity acting as debtor and an entity acting as creditor, under which
25 the creditor normally provides the debtor with funds and the debtor commits to
26 paying them back.
27 Revision mark: clarification is One credit contract can give rise to one or more instruments. Moreover, one credit
added that they may be one or
28 contract may give rise to multiple credit facilities, and each credit facility may give
more instruments under a single
29 contract rise to multiple instruments.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 47
31 May 2019
4.1.2 Instrument
1 In practice, instruments are typically In AnaCredit, credit is understood as any form of financial accommodation supplied
associated with an account
2 by a creditor to a debtor under a credit contract. In this connection, an instrument is a
3 specific instance of credit arising under a contract, with specified characteristics,
4 enabling the debtor to receive from the creditor funds to an amount or value
5 regulated in the contract.
6 AnaCredit considers instruments in the way that they are typically managed by credit
7 institutions, i.e. it considers instruments as banking products with outstanding
8 balances and credit limits.
9 A credit contract may be managed as a whole but may have different characteristics
10 for each instrument.
4.1.2.1 Outstanding balances and credit limits
11 Revision mark: this paragraph has From the perspective of a credit institution, instruments may have a positive or credit

14 A credit limit, in respect of a specific moment in time, is the maximum debit balance
15 allowed on an account at any given moment under the terms of the credit
16 agreement.
17 The debit balance should not at any Credit limits are established during the origination process (approval process) and
time rise above the specified credit
18 are intended to restrict the amount of debt that the creditor extends to a given debtor
limit
19 for the respective credit limit. When a debtor exceeds the credit limits, excesses
20 occur.
21 Outstanding balances as of a given date are the total payments made by or to the
22 debtor on the instrument’s account within a given period, i.e. the balance of the
23 instrument’s account at any point in time is the outstanding balance of the
24 instrument. In other words, outstanding balances are the amount of credit owed by
25 the debtor at a given point in time. For most instruments this is the total amount of
26 drawings and other amounts that are open under the instrument.

4.1.3 Type of instrument
14 The type of instrument is used to classify instruments depending on certain
15 characteristics.
16 Pursuant to Article 1(23) of the AnaCredit Regulation, only those instruments that are
17 specified in the data attribute “type of instrument”, as defined in Annex IV, are
18 considered for AnaCredit reporting. These types are:
19 • Reverse repurchase agreements
20 • Deposits other than reverse repurchase agreements
21 • Overdraft
22 • Credit card debt
23 • Revolving credit other than overdrafts and credit card debt
24 • Credit lines other than revolving credit
25 • Trade receivables
26 • Financial leases
27 • Other loans
28 This means that any instruments which do not fall under any of the types listed
29 above are not considered in AnaCredit.
30 As regards the distinction between the different types of instrument, more detailed
31 guidance is provided in Section 3.4.1 in Part II of the Manual, which deals specifically
32 with the instrument dataset where the data attribute “type of instrument” is

2 the different types of instrument.

17 Pursuant to Article 1(9) of the AnaCredit Regulation, AnaCredit requires that
18 instruments in which an observed agent acts as creditor or servicer are reported.

24 agent acts only as servicer are not automatically considered relevant for reporting.

4.3.1 The activity of the observed agent as creditor
1 The activity of the observed agent as creditor vis-à-vis an instrument implies that the
2 instrument gives rise to credit risk for the observed agent. In this case, the
3 instrument is subject to AnaCredit reporting (irrespective of whether or not the
4 observed agent is the servicer of such instrument) because it fulfils the condition
5 described in Article 4(1)(a)(i) of the AnaCredit Regulation:
(i) gives rise to credit risk for the observed agent.
6 The activity of the observed agent as creditor is covered in cases I and II, as referred
7 to in Section 4.2.
8 Please note that although instruments for which the observed agent acts as creditor
9 generally constitute assets of the observed agent, for the condition in Article
10 4(1)(a)(i) to be fulfilled it is irrelevant whether or not an instrument is an asset of the
11 observed agent under Article 4(1)(a)(ii) and (iii), as long as the observed agent acts
12 as creditor of such instruments.

institutional unit of the legal entity of which the observed agent is part
(these loans are not assets recognised under the accounting standard of
the observed agent’s legal entity);
(b) instruments that are written off, where the observed agent still has a claim
on a third party (debtor) but the instruments are no longer recognised
under the applicable accounting standard.
25 Such instruments are subject to AnaCredit reporting in line with the general criteria
26 triggering the reporting obligation as discussed in Chapter 5, which include the
27 debtor’s commitment amount.
28 As regards written-off instruments in particular, such instruments are reported as
29 long as the debtor’s commitment amount reaches or exceeds the reporting threshold
30 of €25,000, or until the end of the quarter in which instruments were written off if the

4.5.1 Not reporting serviced instruments to avoid double reporting
1 Instruments that are serviced (but not held) by one observed agent and held by
2 another observed agent are in principle subject to double reporting. However, the
3 AnaCredit Regulation aims to avoid double reporting in such cases by requiring in
4 Article 4(1)(a)(iv)(ii) that serviced instruments are reported only on condition that they
5 are not held by another observed agent.
6 More specifically, given the fact that instruments held by observed agents are in
7 principle subject to mandatory AnaCredit reporting, double reporting – which
8 essentially could arise in the case of instruments that are held by one observed
9 agent but serviced by another observed agent – is avoided by exempting the activity
10 of the servicer from reporting.
11 In this connection, the provisions of Article 4(1)(a)(iv)(ii) should be interpreted to mean,
12 in respect of instruments in which a third party acts as creditor, that the activity of an
13 observed agent located in a reporting Member State as servicer to the instrument is
14 subject to reporting to AnaCredit only if the third party acting as creditor to the
15 instrument is not a (part of a) credit institution resident in a reporting Member State.
16 Revision mark: the clarification Example 5 presents a situation in which the data of both the servicer and the creditor
pertaining to Scenario 2 is

expanded
Example 5: Same instrument held by one observed agent and serviced by another
This example considers a fiduciary instrument (cf. Section 4.5.3) from the perspectives
of the trustor and the trustee:
• trustor C holds an instrument which gives rise to credit risk;
• the instrument is serviced by trustee A, who acts as trustee for trustor C;
• counterparty B is the debtor of the instrument; counterparty B is a legal entity.
The fiduciary instrument is first considered from the trustor C perspective:
If trustor C is an observed agent then reporting of the instrument is triggered by Article
4(1)(a)(i) – the instrument gives rise to credit risk for the observed agent.
Conclusion: the reporting agent relevant for the observed agent (trustor C) reports the
instrument.
The instrument is now considered from the trustee A perspective:
Scenario 1 – the instrument is not an asset of trustee A in accordance with the relevant
accounting standard.
If trustee A is an observed agent resident in a reporting Member State, reporting of the
instrument is triggered if trustor C is not a credit institution (or a foreign branch of a
credit institution) resident in a reporting Member State. In such cases reporting is
exclusively triggered by Article 4(1)(a)(iv)(ii).
If trustor C is a credit institution (or a foreign branch of a credit institution) resident in a
reporting Member State, the instrument is not required to be reported by the reporting
agent related to the observed agent (trustee A).
Scenario 2 – the instrument is an asset of trustee A recognised under the applicable
accounting standard.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 60
31 May 2019
If trustee A is an observed agent, reporting of the instrument is triggered by Article
4(1)(a)(ii), irrespective of whether or not trustee A is resident in a reporting Member
State. It is also irrelevant whether or not trustor C is a credit institution (or a foreign
branch of a credit institution) resident in a reporting Member State. When the trustor is
also an observed agent, double reporting to AnaCredit occurs because the same
instrument is reported by both the trustor (in accordance with Article 4(1)(a)(i)) and the
trustee (in accordance Article 4(1)(a)(ii)).

4.5.3 Fiduciary instruments
26 From the perspective of an observed agent, fiduciary instruments are those for which
27 the observed agent acts in its own name but on behalf of and with the risk borne by a
28 third party. The third party who is the economic owner of the instruments is therefore
29 the creditor. The observed agent, who is solely responsible for the administrative and
30 financial management of the instruments, is the servicer of such instruments.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 61
31 May 2019
1 Fiduciary instruments in which the observed agent acts only as servicer are
2 generally subject to AnaCredit reporting. However, the exact reporting obligation
3 depends on their accounting treatment.
4 As discussed in Sections 4.3.2 and 4.5.1, fiduciary instruments may or may not be
5 assets of the trustee. If the trustee is an observed agent, the observed agent acts as
6 servicer but does not act as creditor of such instruments. Fiduciary instruments are
7 therefore reported to AnaCredit according to Article 4(1)(a)(ii) or (iii) of the AnaCredit
8 Regulation if they are treated as assets, regardless of whether or not the creditor is
9 an observed agent.
10 In particular, non-asset fiduciary instruments are only reported to AnaCredit if the
11 servicer (trustee) to such instruments is an observed agent resident in a reporting
12 Member State whereas the creditor (trustor) is not a credit institution (or a foreign
13 branch of a credit institution) resident in a reporting Member State.
14 Please note that only certain data attributes of the accounting dataset are applicable
15 for non-asset fiduciary instruments and proper values can be reported.
4.6 Other instrument-related aspects for consideration
16 This section focuses on certain features of instruments that affect their reporting to
17 AnaCredit.
4.6.1 Instruments recorded on the liabilities side of the balance sheet
18 In accordance with Section 4.4, instruments that give rise to credit risk to the
19 observed agent are either assets of the observed agent or are not recorded on the
20 balance sheet. Such instruments are never liabilities of the observed agent,
21 however.
22 Conversely, any instruments that are of any of the specified types but are a liability of
23 the observed agent are not considered for reporting.
24 Revision mark: the example is Example 6 provides clarification on which deposits are considered and which ones
expanded in line with

Q&A 2018/0014 on nostro/vostro
accounts
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 62
31 May 2019
Example 6: Which deposits are considered and which deposits are not within the
scope of AnaCredit
In accordance with Regulation 549/2013, interbank positions between credit institutions
are generally referred to as deposits. For example, this is mentioned in points 5.81,
5.114, but most notably in point 5.119 thereof.
To this end, although deposits are in principle the type of instrument considered in
AnaCredit, in no case does it imply that any deposits that are recorded on the liabilities
side of the balance sheet fall within the scope of AnaCredit.
As a matter of fact, placements of funds between credit institutions are always recorded
as deposits, irrespective of whether they are on the assets or liabilities side. To be
considered for AnaCredit, however, a deposit should give rise to credit risk for the
observed agent. Specifically, the only deposits that fall within the scope of AnaCredit
are assets in accordance with Article 4(1)(a)(ii) or give rise to credit risk for the observed
agent in accordance with Article 4(1)(a)(i). Consequently, no deposits on the liabilities
side (i.e. received deposits) fall within the scope of the collection.
If, however, the deposit’s balance on the reporting reference data is such that the
observed agent has a claim on the other credit institution – i.e. it is the observed agent’s
funds that are on deposit – the deposit balance is subject to AnaCredit reporting. This is
particularly relevant for nostro/vostro accounts.
A nostro/vostro account is a bank account where one bank has another bank’s funds on
deposit, typically on behalf of a foreign bank in relation to international trade or other
financial transactions. This also involves deposit overdrafts, whereby depositors are
authorised to obtain funding in the form of an overdraft. The terms “nostro” and “vostro”
are used to indicate which bank has money on deposit.
A nostro/vostro account is only subject to AnaCredit reporting if, on the reporting
reference date, the account’s balance is such that the observed agent has a claim on
the other credit institution – i.e. it is the observed agent’s money that is on deposit in the
account (“nostro”). Conversely, “vostro” balances are not subject to AnaCredit reporting.
As the balance of a nostro/vostro account can change from one reporting reference
date to another – changing, for example, from “nostro” to “vostro” and back again – the
account may not be subject to AnaCredit reporting on all reporting reference dates.

1 Revision mark: this paragraph is Instruments which are held or serviced by the observed agent but which are not one

instruments outside the scope of

4 guarantees and commitments given).
5 With this in mind, Example 7 describes an instrument which gives rise to credit risk
6 for the observed agent but is not considered to be subject to AnaCredit reporting.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 63
31 May 2019
Example 7: Financial guarantees given by a credit institution (observed agent)
A credit institution (bank X) whose activity as creditor or servicer is reported by its
reporting agent provides, upon request by company C, a financial guarantee to
company C that if company B, a client of bank X, fails to meet its payment obligation
towards company C, bank X will pay a guaranteed amount to company C.
In this case the guarantee given by bank X is an instrument giving rise to credit risk for
bank X. However, guarantees given are not one of the types of instrument referred to in
Article 1(23) and such instruments therefore fall outside the scope of the collection.
Nevertheless, if company B fails to meet its payment obligation and bank X pays out the
amount guaranteed to company C, then the guarantee becomes a loan for which bank
X acts as creditor (as bank X has the right to receive payment from company B) and is
reported by bank X (in its capacity as an observed agent) (cf. “other loans” in
Section 3.4.1 in Part II of the Manual).

4.6.5.1 Intracompany loans held by the observed agent
11 Revision mark: a definition of Intracompany loans are loans extended by one institutional unit of a legal entity to

15 In the context of AnaCredit, intracompany loans are subject to reporting primarily

19 Intracompany loans may be registered on the balance sheet of the institutional unit,

25 In the AnaCredit Regulation, credit risk is defined at the level of institutional units. In
26 accordance with Article 1(21), credit risk is the risk that a counterparty fails to make
27 any payments that it is contractually obliged to make, while pursuant to Article 1(10)
28 a counterparty is an institutional unit.

AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 66
31 May 2019
Chart 9: Stylised intracompany loans to foreign branches
Intracompany loans are loans extended by one unit of a legal entity to another unit of
the same legal entity. Companies make intracompany loans to, for example, shift cash
within business units that use a common currency, rather than sending in funds from a
foreign location that will be subject to exchange rate fluctuations.
In the context of AnaCredit, intracompany loans within credit institutions are captured at
the level of the institutional unit.
In this example, a credit institution established in France extends intracompany loans to
its foreign branches in Italy, Spain and the USA.

In all these intracompany loans, the creditor is the observed agent located in France,
which is the domestic part of the credit institution. The debtors are the respective foreign
branches that receive the loans. All the intracompany loans are subject to AnaCredit
reporting (provided that they fulfil the conditions set out in Articles 1, 4 and 5 of the
AnaCredit Regulation).
The loans are reported to AnaCredit via the reporting agent on behalf of the observed
agent that is the creditor of these loans, i.e. the institutional unit of the credit institution
that is located in France.

AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 67

Example 8: Reporting of intracompany vostro/nostro accounts
A credit institution runs a nostro/vostro account, which it uses to settle (intracompany)
transactions with its foreign branch.
Initially, on 31 March 2019, the balance of the nostro/vostro account is such that the
domestic part of the credit institution has a claim on the foreign branch (i.e. the account
is a nostro account from the perspective of the domestic part of the credit institution). In
this case, an instrument (i.e. “deposits other than reverse repurchase agreements”) is
reported to AnaCredit, whereby the domestic part is reported as both the creditor and
the servicer and the foreign branch is reported as the debtor.
Later, on 30 April 2019, the balance of the nostro/vostro account is such that the foreign
branch has a claim on the domestic part of the credit institution (i.e. the account is a
nostro account from the perspective of the foreign branch). In this case, the foreign
branch is the observed agent and is reported as the creditor and the domestic part is
reported as both the debtor and the servicer).

3 subject to AnaCredit reporting because these are loans made within a single
4 institutional unit. This is because units of a single entity located in a single country
5 form a single institutional unit, and any transactions within institutional units are not
6 recognised in AnaCredit.

4.6.6 The commencement of the reporting of an instrument
21 The following guidelines are considered when establishing the moment at which an
22 instrument becomes relevant for AnaCredit reporting.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 68
31 May 2019
1 Generally, an instrument first becomes subject to reporting at the moment at which
2 the creditor enables the debtor to draw funds after entering into a legally binding
3 agreement with the debtor, or when funds are disbursed to the debtor for the first
4 time in the case of overdrafts that are not associated with an agreed credit limit.
5 Moreover, whether the creditor makes a commitment to the debtor in irrevocable or
6 revocable15 terms is irrelevant for the moment of commencement of the reporting of
7 an instrument.
8 Revision mark: this section is In the case of structured instruments under multi‑debtor/product umbrellas, such
expanded in line with
9 instruments, irrespective of their structural complexity, become subject to reporting at
Q&A 2018/0010
10 the moment at which the creditor, at or after the moment of contractual inception and
11 after communicating and legally defining the commitment, creates an eligible
12 instrument giving the debtor the possibility of taking advantage of funds. This could,
13 for example, be a credit limit (regardless of whether it has been communicated to the
14 debtor) which has not resulted in the creation of an eligible instrument (e.g. a credit
15 card account or an overdraft). Clearly, such a commitment/offer will not result in any
16 reporting to AnaCredit until a standby instrument has been created which may be
17 utilised by the debtor solely at the debtor’s discretion. The same is true of conditional
18 lending agreements prior to the creation of an eligible instrument that is subject to
19 AnaCredit reporting (cf. Part III of the Manual, which deals specifically with
20 instruments arising under off-balance-sheet items).
21 For more information regarding the inception of reportable instruments, please refer
22 to Section 3.1.5 in Part II of the Manual.

15 Instruments giving rise to credit risk can generally be either irrevocable or revocable, based on
the following definitions:
• “irrevocable” refers to any instrument where the credit institution confirms to the debtor in
irrevocable and legally binding terms that it is prepared to make available a certain amount
(credit limit) during a predefined period of time, subject to certain terms and conditions stipulated
in the credit contract;
• “revocable” refers to any instrument (documented in a credit agreement) which can be
cancelled or drawing requests refused at any time at the sole discretion of the credit institution,
and which the credit institution has no legal obligation to renew beyond its original tenor.
Please note that the “irrevocable” effect is reflected to the extent that, once the debtor complies
with the terms and conditions (including loan default clauses and conditions precedent), the
credit institution has no legal right to unilaterally refuse any drawing and/or demand any
prepayment and/or cancel the facility until the (legally binding) contract underpinning the
instrument has expired.

3 The existence of such instruments is often a result of the legal process of liquidating
4 a company, which is complicated and may last for many years, and there may be
5 conflicting views about whether or not a counterparty has ceased to exist. In such
6 cases, there is a possibility to centrally consolidate the different views of different
7 reporting agents (via the Register of Institutions and Affiliates Data – RIAD)16,
8 whereas AnaCredit holds information about such debtors’ current exposures (which
9 may be of particular relevance in the context of non-performing loans).
16 The ECB maintains the Register of Institutions and Affiliates Data (RIAD). RIAD is the shared
dataset of reference data on legal and other statistical institutional units, the collection of which
supports business processes across the Eurosystem and the performance of the tasks of the
ESCB and the SSM. RIAD facilitates the integration of a variety of datasets, in particular by
providing common identifiers.

Chart 10: Establishing whether an instrument is required to be reported as of a
reporting reference date
To establish whether a (written-off) instrument is required to be reported as of a
reporting reference date:
• The reporting agent checks whether the instrument is a qualifying instrument, i.e. it
fulfils the respective conditions of Articles 4 and 5 for the corresponding month-end
date. 1
• If the instrument fulfils the respective conditions on the reporting reference date
(qualifying instrument), then the instrument is required to be reported as of the
reporting reference date.
• If the instrument does not meet the respective conditions, the reporting agent
checks whether the instrument is subject to extended quarter-end reporting. 2
• If extended quarter-end reporting applies, the instrument is reported as of the
reporting reference date
• Otherwise, the instrument is not required to be reported as of the reporting
reference date.

7 The conditions are that the instrument:
(a) gives rise to credit risk for the observed agent, or
(b) is an asset of the observed agent, or
(c) is recognised under the relevant accounting standard used by the
observed agent’s legal entity and gave rise to credit risk for the observed

9 held or serviced by the observed agent is only considered if at least one debtor of
10 the instrument is a legal entity or part of a legal entity as defined in Article 1(5).
11 Revision mark: this paragraph is Otherwise, if the only debtor (or all debtors if there are more than one) of the

18 The following examples provide an illustration of how condition C is applied.
Example 9: Loan granted to an investment fund
A loan held by the observed agent, classed as “other loans” according to the type of
instrument under Article 1(23) and granted to an investment fund fulfils condition C
above. The investment fund is a debtor of the loan.
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 77
31 May 2019
Example 10: Credit card debt granted to a natural person
A credit card debt granted by the observed agent, classified as “credit card debt”
according to the type of instrument under Article 1(23) and granted to a natural person
does not to fulfil condition C above.
Example 11: Revolving credit granted to a partnership
A revolving credit granted by the observed agent, classified as “revolving credit other
than overdrafts and credit card debt” according to the type of instrument under Article
1(23) and granted to a partnership is deemed to fulfil condition C above.
The partnership is set up by a deed of partnership, which sets out the legal relationship
between partners (how profits will be shared out, responsibilities of partners, etc.). The
partners have unlimited liability, i.e. they are jointly and severely responsible for the
debts of their partnership. The partnership is a legal entity as defined in Article 1(5) of
the AnaCredit Regulation.
Example 12: Loan granted to a natural person and a company
A mortgage loan granted by the observed agent, classified as “other loans” according to
the type of instrument under Article 1(23) and granted to a natural person and a private
company, which are jointly liable for the debt, is deemed to fulfil condition C above.

For a given banking product, the type of instrument is determined based on the
definitions provided in Annex IV of the AnaCredit Regulation in the data attribute “type
of instrument”, to which Article 1(23) refers. In fact, the type of instrument is actually
determined on the basis of a number of features (laid down in the definitions of the
individual types of instrument) which go beyond the brief description provided here for
illustrative purposes.
Please note that for some banking products which are held or serviced by banks, there
may not be an appropriate type of instrument because it may not be covered by the
scope of the collection as laid down in the AnaCredit Regulation. For example, a
financial guarantee given by a bank does not have a corresponding type of instrument
in Annex IV.17
If any of the three eligibility conditions is not fulfilled, the instrument held or serviced by
the bank is not eligible. For example:
• a financial guarantee (Case 1) is not eligible because it does not correspond to any
of the type of instrument laid down in Annex IV of the AnaCredit Regulation;
• a term loan (Case 4) may not be eligible if the only debtor of the loan is a natural
person;
• an over-the-counter (OTC) derivative (Case 7) is not eligible because it does not
correspond to any of the typed of instrument laid down in Annex IV.
Please note that whether or not a particular condition is fulfilled depends on a number of
other features which are not necessarily mentioned in this description.

This example concerns the calculation of the debtor’s commitment amount in relation to
instruments other than instruments governed under a cross-limit.

Instrument 4 Eligible 12,000.00 0.00 12,000.00
Two of the instruments are deemed eligible, the other two instruments are deemed not

To determine whether the reporting threshold referred to in Article 5 is reached or
exceeded, the debtor’s commitment amount is established by adding the commitment
amounts of the two eligible instruments.
The outstanding nominal amount and off-balance-sheet amount are added together for
each instrument separately. This sum is referred to as the commitment amount of the
instrument.
The debtor’s commitment amount in respect of all eligible instruments is established as
follows:
• all eligible instruments in which the debtor is involved are identified; these are
Instruments 2 and 4;
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 81
31 May 2019
• the commitment amounts of the instruments identified as eligible in the previous
step are €30,000 for Instrument 2 and €12,000 for Instrument 4;
• the two instruments that are not eligible are not considered in the calculation of the
debtor’s commitment amount vis-à-vis the observed agent;
• the debtor’s commitment amount is determined to be €42,000 (€30,000 + €12,000),
as effectively only Instruments 2 and 4 are considered.
Since the debtor’s commitment amount exceeds the reporting threshold of €25,000,
Instruments 2 and 4 are qualifying instruments as of the month-end.

Example 15: Commitment amount in the case of a multi-debtor situation
This example concerns the calculation of the debtor’s commitment amount in the case
of multi-debtor instruments, where the commitment of one of the debtors is below the
reporting threshold.
As of the month-end there is an Instrument 1 with two debtors: legal entity A and legal
entity B.
Legal entity A is a debtor of two eligible instruments vis-à-vis the observed agent:
Instrument 1 and Instrument 2. The commitment amount of Instrument 1 is €15,000 and
the commitment amount of Instrument 2 is €12,000. There are no other instruments of
the debtor vis-à-vis the observed agent.
Consequently, the debtor’s commitment amount of legal entity A exceeds €25,000.
This means that both eligible instruments (Instrument 1 and Instrument 2) of legal entity
A fall within the scope of AnaCredit and need to be reported.
With regard to legal entity B, the debtor has only one eligible instrument vis-à-vis the
observed agent, i.e. Instrument 1. The debtor’s commitment amount of legal entity B is
AnaCredit Reporting Manual – Part I – General methodology 82
31 May 2019
therefore €12,000 and does not exceed the reporting threshold of €25,000.
However, as Instrument 1 is subject to reporting (based on the debtor’s commitment of
legal entity A, legal entity B is required to be reported to AnaCredit in relation to this
instrument – in fact, both legal entity A and legal entity B are required to be reported in
the counterparty-instrument dataset as debtors to Instrument 1.
In addition, since both debtors are registered in the counterparty-instrument dataset, the
counterparty reference data is also reported for both legal entity A and legal entity B.

Client B is the only debtor of Instruments 6, 7 and 9 and is one of the debtors of
Instrument 5 (i.e. there is a plurality of debtors). Client C is the only debtor of
Instruments 8 and 10 and is one of the debtors of Instrument 5. There are no other
instruments vis-à-vis the observed agent in which these clients are debtors.
To determine whether the eligible instruments held or serviced by the observed agent
reach or exceed the threshold referred to in Article 5, the debtor’s commitment amount
is established for these instruments, taking into account that there are two debtors of
Instrument 5.
For client B, one of debtors of Instrument 5, the debtor’s commitment amount is
established as follows:
i. all eligible instruments in which client B is involved are identified (Instruments 5
to 7);
ii. the commitment amounts of the identified instruments are €9,000 for
Instrument 5, €3,500 for Instrument 6 and €15,000 for Instrument 7;
iii. Instrument 9 is not considered as it is not eligible;
iv. the debtor’s commitment amount for client B is determined to be €27,500
(€9,000 + €3,500 + €15,000).
For client C, the other debtor of Instrument 5, the debtor’s commitment amount is
established as follows:
i. all eligible instruments in which client C is involved are identified (Instruments 5
and 8);
ii. the commitment amounts of the identified instruments are €9,000 for
Instrument 5 and €5,000 for Instrument 8;
iii. Instrument 10 is not considered as it is not eligible;
iv. the debtor’s commitment amount for client C is determined to be €14,000
(€9,000 + €5,000).
Instrument 5 is therefore a qualifying instrument as of the month-end, as are
Instruments 6 and 7. In this case only client B’s commitment amount exceeds €25,000
and all instruments relating to client B are reported, including client C’s involvement in
Instrument 5.
However, Instrument 8 of client C (although it is an eligible instrument) is not a
qualifying instrument as of the month-end because the debtor’s commitment amount of
client C is lower than €25,000.

5.2.2 Verifying whether extended quarter-end reporting applies
3 Revision mark: this section is The use of the reference period in Article 5(1) of the AnaCredit Regulation implies

4 that instruments are reported until at least the end of the quarter in which the

6 extended quarter-end reporting is of particular relevance for written-off instruments
7 and is aimed at capturing the total write-off amount via the accounting dataset as of
8 the quarter-end reporting reference date.
9 The use of the reference period when setting out the requirements in Articles 4 and 5
10 of the AnaCredit Regulation in combination with extended quarter-end reporting
11 ensures that each qualifying instrument that incurs a write-off is reported at least until
12 the end of the calendar quarter in which the debtor’s commitment amount falls below
13 the reporting threshold (as a result of the write-off).
14 By maintaining the reporting obligation for written-off instruments until the quarter-

16 otherwise remain uncaptured. For example, the total write-off amount relating to the
17 instrument is captured in AnaCredit via the accounting dataset as of the quarter-end
18 reporting reference date.

7 credit institution holds the instrument and there is a counterparty (the debtor) which
8 has an obligation to pay and, as long as the write-off does not release the debtor
9 from the obligation, the credit institution is a creditor of the instrument and bears the
10 credit risk of the instrument.
